<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Artificial intelligence</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/><img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Artificial_intelligence">reviewed</a> on <i>2 October 2016</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="hatnote" role="note">"AI" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/AI_(disambiguation)" title="AI (disambiguation)">AI</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_(disambiguation)" title="Artificial intelligence (disambiguation)">Artificial intelligence (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p><b>Artificial intelligence</b> (<b>AI</b>) is <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">intelligence</a> exhibited by <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">machines</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, an ideal "intelligent" machine is a flexible <a href="/wiki/Rational_agent" title="Rational agent">rational agent</a> that perceives its environment and takes actions that maximize its chance of success at some goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intelligent_agents_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Intelligent_agents-1">[1]</a></sup> Colloquially, the term "artificial intelligence" is applied when a machine mimics "cognitive" functions that humans associate with other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mind" title="Human mind">human minds</a>, such as "learning" and "problem solving".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20092_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20092-2">[2]</a></sup> As machines become increasingly capable, facilities once thought to require intelligence are removed from the definition. For example, <a href="/wiki/Optical_character_recognition" title="Optical character recognition">optical character recognition</a> is no longer perceived as an exemplar of "artificial intelligence" having become a routine technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Capabilities currently classified as AI include successfully <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">understanding human speech</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2009_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2009-4">[4]</a></sup> competing at a high level in strategic game systems (such as <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">Chess</a> and <a href="/wiki/Go_(game)" title="Go (game)">Go</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-alphago_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-alphago-5">[5]</a></sup>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-driving_cars" title="Self-driving cars">self-driving cars</a>, and interpreting complex data.</p>
<p>AI research is divided into subfields<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fragmentation_of_AI_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fragmentation_of_AI-6">[6]</a></sup> that focus on specific <a href="#Goals">problems</a> or on specific <a href="#Approaches">approaches</a> or on the use of a particular <a href="#Tools">tool</a> or towards satisfying particular <a href="#Applications">applications</a>.</p>
<p>The central problems (or goals) of AI research include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">natural language processing</a> (communication), <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a> and the ability to move and manipulate objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Problems_of_AI-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">General intelligence</a> is among the field's long-term goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-General_intelligence_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-General_intelligence-8">[8]</a></sup> Approaches include <a href="#Statistical">statistical methods</a>, <a href="#Sub-symbolic">computational intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soft_computing" title="Soft computing">soft computing</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a>), and <a href="#Symbolic">traditional symbolic AI</a>. Many tools are used in AI, including versions of <a href="#Search_and_optimization">search and mathematical optimization</a>, <a href="#Logic">logic</a>, <a href="#Probabilistic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning">methods based on probability and economics</a>. The AI field draws upon <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_psychology" title="Artificial psychology">artificial psychology</a>.</p>
<p>The field was founded on the claim that <a href="/wiki/Human_intelligence" title="Human intelligence">human intelligence</a> "can be so precisely described that a machine can be made to simulate it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> This raises philosophical arguments about the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> and the ethics of creating artificial beings endowed with human-like intelligence, issues which have been explored by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_AI#AI_in_myth.2C_fiction_and_speculation" title="History of AI">myth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_fiction" title="Artificial intelligence in fiction">fiction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_AI" title="Philosophy of AI">philosophy</a> since <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCorduck.27s_thesis_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCorduck.27s_thesis-10">[10]</a></sup> Attempts to create artificial intelligence has experienced many <a href="/wiki/AI_winter" title="AI winter">setbacks</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/AI_winter#Machine_translation_and_the_ALPAC_report_of_1966" title="AI winter">ALPAC report</a> of 1966, the abandonment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perceptrons" title="Perceptrons">perceptrons</a> in 1970, <a href="/wiki/AI_winter#The_Lighthill_report" title="AI winter">the Lighthill Report</a> of 1973 and the <a href="/wiki/AI_winter#The_collapse_of_the_Lisp_machine_market_in_1987" title="AI winter">collapse of the Lisp machine market</a> in 1987. In the twenty-first century AI techniques became an essential part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technology_industry" title="Technology industry">technology industry</a>, helping to solve many challenging problems in computer science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_widely_used_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_widely_used-11">[11]</a></sup></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence" title="History of artificial intelligence">History of artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Timeline of artificial intelligence">Timeline of artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>While the concept of thought capable artificial beings appeared as storytelling devices in antiquity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_in_myth_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_myth-12">[12]</a></sup> the idea of actually trying to build a machine to perform useful reasoning may have begun with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramon_Lull" title="Ramon Lull">Ramon Lull</a> (c. 1300 CE). With his <a href="/wiki/Calculus_ratiocinator" title="Calculus ratiocinator">Calculus ratiocinator</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a> extended the concept of the calculating machine (<a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Schickard" title="Wilhelm Schickard">Wilhelm Schickard</a> engineered the first one around 1623), intending to perform operations on concepts rather than numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200916_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200916-13">[13]</a></sup> Since the 19th century, artificial beings are common in fiction, as in <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i> or <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel Čapek">Karel Čapek</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R.U.R._(Rossum%27s_Universal_Robots)" title="R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots)">R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots)</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_in_early_science_fiction_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_early_science_fiction-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The study of mechanical or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_reasoning" title="Formal reasoning">"formal" reasoning</a> began with philosophers and mathematicians in antiquity. In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/George_Boole" title="George Boole">George Boole</a> refined those ideas into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propositional_logic" title="Propositional logic">propositional logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a> developed a notational system for mechanical reasoning (a <i>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predicate_calculus" title="Predicate calculus">predicate calculus</a>"</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENilsson1998Section_1.3_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENilsson1998Section_1.3-15">[15]</a></sup> Around the 1940s, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_computation" title="Theory of computation">theory of computation</a> suggested that a machine, by shuffling symbols as simple as "0" and "1", could simulate any conceivable act of mathematical deduction. This insight, that digital computers can simulate any process of formal reasoning, is known as the <a href="/wiki/Church%E2%80%93Turing_thesis" title="Church–Turing thesis">Church–Turing thesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Formal_reasoning_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Formal_reasoning-16">[16]</a></sup> Along with concurrent discoveries in <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cybernetic" title="Cybernetic">cybernetics</a>, this led researchers to consider the possibility of building an electronic brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI.27s_immediate_precursors_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI.27s_immediate_precursors-17">[17]</a></sup> The first work that is now generally recognized as AI was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warren_McCullough" title="Warren McCullough">McCullough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Pitts" title="Walter Pitts">Pitts</a>' 1943 formal design for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turing-complete" title="Turing-complete">Turing-complete</a> "artificial neurons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200916_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200916-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The field of AI research was founded at <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Conferences" title="Dartmouth Conferences">a conference</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a> in 1956.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_conference_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_conference-18">[18]</a></sup> The attendees, including <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">John McCarthy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allen_Newell" title="Allen Newell">Allen Newell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Samuel" title="Arthur Samuel">Arthur Samuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon" title="Herbert A. Simon">Herbert Simon</a>, became the leaders of AI research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hegemony_of_the_Dartmouth_conference_attendees_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hegemony_of_the_Dartmouth_conference_attendees-19">[19]</a></sup> They and their students wrote programs that were, to most people, simply astonishing:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200318_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200318-20">[20]</a></sup> computers were winning at checkers, solving word problems in algebra, proving logical theorems and speaking English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golden_years_of_AI_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_years_of_AI-21">[21]</a></sup> By the middle of the 1960s, research in the U.S. was heavily funded by the <a href="/wiki/DARPA" title="DARPA">Department of Defense</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_funding_in_the_60s_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_funding_in_the_60s-22">[22]</a></sup> and laboratories had been established around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_in_England_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_England-23">[23]</a></sup> AI's founders were optimistic about the future: <a href="/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon" title="Herbert A. Simon">Herbert Simon</a> predicted that "machines will be capable, within twenty years, of doing any work a man can do". <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a> agreed, writing that "within a generation ... the problem of creating 'artificial intelligence' will substantially be solved".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Optimism_of_early_AI_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Optimism_of_early_AI-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>They failed to recognize the difficulty of some of the remaining tasks. Progress slowed and in 1974, in response to the criticism of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_James_Lighthill" title="Sir James Lighthill">Sir James Lighthill</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELighthill1973_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELighthill1973-25">[25]</a></sup> and ongoing pressure from the US Congress to fund more productive projects, both the U.S. and British governments cut off exploratory research in AI. The next few years would later be called an "<a href="/wiki/AI_winter" title="AI winter">AI winter</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-First_AI_winter_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-First_AI_winter-26">[26]</a></sup> a period when funding for AI projects was hard to find.</p>
<p>In the early 1980s, AI research was revived by the commercial success of <a href="/wiki/Expert_system" title="Expert system">expert systems</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Expert_systems_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Expert_systems-27">[27]</a></sup> a form of AI program that simulated the knowledge and analytical skills of human experts. By 1985 the market for AI had reached over a billion dollars. At the same time, Japan's <a href="/wiki/Fifth_generation_computer" title="Fifth generation computer">fifth generation computer</a> project inspired the U.S and British governments to restore funding for academic research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_in_the_80s_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_the_80s-28">[28]</a></sup> However, beginning with the collapse of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lisp_Machine" title="Lisp Machine">Lisp Machine</a> market in 1987, AI once again fell into disrepute, and a second, longer-lasting hiatus began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Second_AI_winter_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Second_AI_winter-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1990s and early 21st century, AI began to be used for logistics, <a href="/wiki/Data_mining" title="Data mining">data mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">medical diagnosis</a> and other areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_widely_used_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-AI_widely_used-11">[11]</a></sup> The success was due to increasing computational power (see <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a>), greater emphasis on solving specific problems, new ties between AI and other fields and a commitment by researchers to mathematical methods and scientific standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Formal_methods_in_AI_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Formal_methods_in_AI-30">[30]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IBM_Deep_Blue" title="IBM Deep Blue">Deep Blue</a> became the first computer chess-playing system to beat a reigning world chess champion, <a href="/wiki/Garry_Kasparov" title="Garry Kasparov">Garry Kasparov</a> on 11 May 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004480.E2.80.93483_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004480.E2.80.93483-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Advanced statistical techniques (loosely known as <a href="/wiki/Deep_learning" title="Deep learning">deep learning</a>), access to <a href="/wiki/Big_data" title="Big data">large amounts of data</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">faster computers</a> enabled advances in <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> and perception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deep_learning_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-deep_learning-32">[32]</a></sup> By the mid 2010s, machine learning applications were used throughout the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-success_in_21st_century_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-success_in_21st_century-33">[33]</a></sup> In a <i><a href="/wiki/Jeopardy!" title="Jeopardy!">Jeopardy!</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quiz_show" title="Quiz show">quiz show</a> exhibition match, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Question_answering_system" title="Question answering system">question answering system</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watson_(artificial_intelligence_software)" title="Watson (artificial intelligence software)">Watson</a>, defeated the two greatest Jeopardy champions, <a href="/wiki/Brad_Rutter" title="Brad Rutter">Brad Rutter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ken_Jennings" title="Ken Jennings">Ken Jennings</a>, by a significant margin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkoff2011_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkoff2011-34">[34]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kinect" title="Kinect">Kinect</a>, which provides a 3D body–motion interface for the <a href="/wiki/Xbox_360" title="Xbox 360">Xbox 360</a> and the Xbox One use algorithms that emerged from lengthy AI research<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> as do <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_personal_assistant" title="Intelligent personal assistant">intelligent personal assistants</a> in <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In March 2016, <a href="/wiki/AlphaGo" title="AlphaGo">AlphaGo</a> won 4 out of 5 games of <a href="/wiki/Go_(game)" title="Go (game)">Go</a> in a match with Go champion <a href="/wiki/Lee_Sedol" title="Lee Sedol">Lee Sedol</a>, becoming the first <a href="/wiki/Computer_Go" title="Computer Go">computer Go-playing system</a> to beat a professional Go player without <a href="/wiki/Go_handicaps" title="Go handicaps">handicaps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-alphago_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-alphago-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Goals">Goals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Goals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The general problem of simulating (or creating) intelligence has been broken down into sub-problems. These consist of particular traits or capabilities that researchers expect an intelligent system to display. The traits described below have received the most attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Problems_of_AI-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deduction.2C_reasoning.2C_problem_solving">Deduction, reasoning, problem solving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Deduction, reasoning, problem solving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Early researchers developed algorithms that imitated step-by-step reasoning that humans use when they solve puzzles or make logical deductions (reason).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reasoning_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reasoning-38">[38]</a></sup> By the late 1980s and 1990s, AI research had developed methods for dealing with <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty" title="Uncertainty">uncertain</a> or incomplete information, employing concepts from <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uncertain_reasoning_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uncertain_reasoning-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>For difficult problems, algorithms can require enormous computational resources—most experience a "<a href="/wiki/Combinatorial_explosion" title="Combinatorial explosion">combinatorial explosion</a>": the amount of memory or computer time required becomes astronomical for problems of a certain size. The search for more efficient problem-solving algorithms is a high priority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intractability_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Intractability-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Human beings ordinarily use fast, intuitive judgments rather than step-by-step deduction that early AI research was able to model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning-41">[41]</a></sup> AI has progressed using "sub-symbolic" problem solving: <a href="/wiki/Embodied_agent" title="Embodied agent">embodied agent</a> approaches emphasize the importance of <a href="/wiki/Sensory-motor_coupling" title="Sensory-motor coupling">sensorimotor</a> skills to higher reasoning; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_net" title="Neural net">neural net</a> research attempts to simulate the structures inside the brain that give rise to this skill; <a href="#Statistical">statistical approaches to AI</a> mimic the human ability to guess.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Knowledge_representation">Knowledge representation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Knowledge representation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GFO_taxonomy_tree.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="469" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/GFO_taxonomy_tree.png/220px-GFO_taxonomy_tree.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/GFO_taxonomy_tree.png/330px-GFO_taxonomy_tree.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/GFO_taxonomy_tree.png/440px-GFO_taxonomy_tree.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GFO_taxonomy_tree.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An ontology represents knowledge as a set of concepts within a domain and the relationships between those concepts.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation">Knowledge representation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge" title="Commonsense knowledge">Commonsense knowledge</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation">Knowledge representation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knowledge_representation_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_representation-42">[42]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_engineering" title="Knowledge engineering">knowledge engineering</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knowledge_engineering_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_engineering-43">[43]</a></sup> are central to AI research. Many of the problems machines are expected to solve will require extensive knowledge about the world. Among the things that AI needs to represent are: objects, properties, categories and relations between objects;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Representing_categories_and_relations_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_categories_and_relations-44">[44]</a></sup> situations, events, states and time;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Representing_time_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_time-45">[45]</a></sup> causes and effects;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Representing_causation_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_causation-46">[46]</a></sup> knowledge about knowledge (what we know about what other people know);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge-47">[47]</a></sup> and many other, less well researched domains. A representation of "what exists" is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontology_(computer_science)" title="Ontology (computer science)">ontology</a>: the set of objects, relations, concepts and so on that the machine knows about. The most general are called <a href="/wiki/Upper_ontology" title="Upper ontology">upper ontologies</a>, which attempt to provide a foundation for all other knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ontology_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ontology-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the most difficult problems in knowledge representation are:</p>
<dl>
<dt><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Default_reasoning" title="Default reasoning">Default reasoning</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Qualification_problem" title="Qualification problem">qualification problem</a></dt>
<dd>Many of the things people know take the form of "working assumptions." For example, if a bird comes up in conversation, people typically picture an animal that is fist sized, sings, and flies. None of these things are true about all birds. <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">John McCarthy</a> identified this problem in 1969<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qualification_problem_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qualification_problem-49">[49]</a></sup> as the qualification problem: for any commonsense rule that AI researchers care to represent, there tend to be a huge number of exceptions. Almost nothing is simply true or false in the way that abstract logic requires. AI research has explored a number of solutions to this problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic-50">[50]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>The breadth of commonsense knowledge</dt>
<dd>The number of atomic facts that the average person knows is astronomical. Research projects that attempt to build a complete knowledge base of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge" title="Commonsense knowledge">commonsense knowledge</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cyc" title="Cyc">Cyc</a>) require enormous amounts of laborious <a href="/wiki/Ontology_engineering" title="Ontology engineering">ontological engineering</a>—they must be built, by hand, one complicated concept at a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breadth_of_commonsense_knowledge_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Breadth_of_commonsense_knowledge-51">[51]</a></sup> A major goal is to have the computer understand enough concepts to be able to learn by reading from sources like the Internet, and thus be able to add to its own ontology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>The subsymbolic form of some commonsense knowledge</dt>
<dd>Much of what people know is not represented as "facts" or "statements" that they could express verbally. For example, a chess master will avoid a particular chess position because it "feels too exposed"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDreyfusDreyfus1986_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyfusDreyfus1986-52">[52]</a></sup> or an art critic can take one look at a statue and instantly realize that it is a fake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGladwell2005_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGladwell2005-53">[53]</a></sup> These are intuitions or tendencies that are represented in the brain non-consciously and sub-symbolically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intuition_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Intuition-54">[54]</a></sup> Knowledge like this informs, supports and provides a context for symbolic, conscious knowledge. As with the related problem of sub-symbolic reasoning, it is hoped that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Situated_artificial_intelligence" title="Situated artificial intelligence">situated AI</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computational_intelligence" title="Computational intelligence">computational intelligence</a>, or <a href="#Statistical">statistical AI</a> will provide ways to represent this kind of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intuition_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Intuition-54">[54]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Planning">Planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hierarchical-control-system.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="539" data-file-width="596" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/76/Hierarchical-control-system.svg/220px-Hierarchical-control-system.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/76/Hierarchical-control-system.svg/330px-Hierarchical-control-system.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/76/Hierarchical-control-system.svg/440px-Hierarchical-control-system.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hierarchical-control-system.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_control_system" title="Hierarchical control system">hierarchical control system</a> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Control_system" title="Control system">control system</a> in which a set of devices and governing software is arranged in a hierarchy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">Automated planning and scheduling</a></div>
<p>Intelligent agents must be able to set goals and achieve them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planning_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planning-55">[55]</a></sup> They need a way to visualize the future (they must have a representation of the state of the world and be able to make predictions about how their actions will change it) and be able to make choices that maximize the <a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a> (or "value") of the available choices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Information_value_theory_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Information_value_theory-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In classical planning problems, the agent can assume that it is the only thing acting on the world and it can be certain what the consequences of its actions may be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Classical_planning_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Classical_planning-57">[57]</a></sup> However, if the agent is not the only actor, it must periodically ascertain whether the world matches its predictions and it must change its plan as this becomes necessary, requiring the agent to reason under uncertainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Non-deterministic_planning_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Non-deterministic_planning-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Multi-agent_planning" title="Multi-agent planning">Multi-agent planning</a> uses the <a href="/wiki/Cooperation" title="Cooperation">cooperation</a> and competition of many agents to achieve a given goal. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergent_behavior" title="Emergent behavior">Emergent behavior</a> such as this is used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_algorithms" title="Evolutionary algorithms">evolutionary algorithms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swarm_intelligence" title="Swarm intelligence">swarm intelligence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Multi-agent_planning_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Multi-agent_planning-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Learning">Learning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Learning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">Machine learning</a></div>
<p>Machine learning is the study of computer algorithms that improve automatically through experience<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Machine_learning_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Machine_learning-61">[61]</a></sup> and has been central to AI research since the field's inception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Unsupervised_learning" title="Unsupervised learning">Unsupervised learning</a> is the ability to find patterns in a stream of input. <a href="/wiki/Supervised_learning" title="Supervised learning">Supervised learning</a> includes both <a href="/wiki/Statistical_classification" title="Statistical classification">classification</a> and numerical <a href="/wiki/Regression_analysis" title="Regression analysis">regression</a>. Classification is used to determine what category something belongs in, after seeing a number of examples of things from several categories. Regression is the attempt to produce a function that describes the relationship between inputs and outputs and predicts how the outputs should change as the inputs change. In <a href="/wiki/Reinforcement_learning" title="Reinforcement learning">reinforcement learning</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinforcement_learning_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reinforcement_learning-63">[63]</a></sup> the agent is rewarded for good responses and punished for bad ones. The agent uses this sequence of rewards and punishments to form a strategy for operating in its problem space. These three types of learning can be analyzed in terms of <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, using concepts like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utility_(economics)" title="Utility (economics)">utility</a>. The mathematical analysis of machine learning algorithms and their performance is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">theoretical computer science</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Computational_learning_theory" title="Computational learning theory">computational learning theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Computational_learning_theory_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Computational_learning_theory-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within <a href="/wiki/Developmental_robotics" title="Developmental robotics">developmental robotics</a>, developmental learning approaches were elaborated for lifelong cumulative acquisition of repertoires of novel skills by a robot, through autonomous self-exploration and social interaction with human teachers, and using guidance mechanisms such as active learning, maturation, motor synergies, and imitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWengMcClellandPentlandSporns2001_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWengMcClellandPentlandSporns2001-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELungarellaMettaPfeiferSandini2003_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELungarellaMettaPfeiferSandini2003-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsadaHosodaKuniyoshiIshiguro2009_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsadaHosodaKuniyoshiIshiguro2009-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOudeyer2010_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOudeyer2010-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_language_processing_.28communication.29">Natural language processing (communication)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Natural language processing (communication)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ParseTree.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="120" data-file-width="120" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/ParseTree.svg/220px-ParseTree.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/ParseTree.svg/330px-ParseTree.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/ParseTree.svg/440px-ParseTree.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ParseTree.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Parse_tree" title="Parse tree">parse tree</a> represents the <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntactic</a> structure of a sentence according to some <a href="/wiki/Formal_grammar" title="Formal grammar">formal grammar</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">Natural language processing</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">Natural language processing</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_language_processing_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_language_processing-69">[69]</a></sup> gives machines the ability to read and <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_understanding" title="Natural language understanding">understand</a> the languages that humans speak. A sufficiently powerful natural language processing system would enable <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_user_interface" title="Natural language user interface">natural language user interfaces</a> and the acquisition of knowledge directly from human-written sources, such as newswire texts. Some straightforward applications of natural language processing include <a href="/wiki/Information_retrieval" title="Information retrieval">information retrieval</a>, <a href="/wiki/Text_mining" title="Text mining">text mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Question_answering" title="Question answering">question answering</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">machine translation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applications_of_natural_language_processing_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Applications_of_natural_language_processing-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>A common method of processing and extracting meaning from natural language is through semantic indexing. Increases in processing speeds and the drop in the cost of data storage makes indexing large volumes of abstractions of the user's input much more efficient.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Perception">Perception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Perception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">Machine perception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">Computer vision</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">Speech recognition</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">Machine perception</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Machine_perception_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Machine_perception-72">[72]</a></sup> is the ability to use input from sensors (such as cameras, microphones, <a href="/wiki/Tactile_sensor" title="Tactile sensor">tactile sensors</a>, sonar and others more exotic) to deduce aspects of the world. <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">Computer vision</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Computer_vision_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Computer_vision-73">[73]</a></sup> is the ability to analyze visual input. A few selected subproblems are <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">speech recognition</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Speech_recognition_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Speech_recognition-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Facial_recognition_system" title="Facial recognition system">facial recognition</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Object_recognition" title="Object recognition">object recognition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Object_recognition_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Object_recognition-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Motion_and_manipulation">Motion and manipulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Motion and manipulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">Robotics</a></div>
<p>The field of <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robotics_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robotics-76">[76]</a></sup> is closely related to AI. Intelligence is required for robots to be able to handle such tasks as object manipulation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Configuration_space_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Configuration_space-77">[77]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Motion_planning" title="Motion planning">navigation</a>, with sub-problems of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robot_localization" title="Robot localization">localization</a> (knowing where you are, or finding out where other things are), <a href="/wiki/Robotic_mapping" title="Robotic mapping">mapping</a> (learning what is around you, building a map of the environment), and <a href="/wiki/Motion_planning" title="Motion planning">motion planning</a> (figuring out how to get there) or path planning (going from one point in space to another point, which may involve compliant motion – where the robot moves while maintaining physical contact with an object).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETecuci2012_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETecuci2012-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robotic_mapping_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robotic_mapping-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_goals">Long-term goals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Long-term goals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Among the long-term goals in the research pertaining to artificial intelligence are: (1) Social intelligence, (2) Creativity, and (3) General intelligence.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_intelligence">Social intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Social intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Affective_computing" title="Affective computing">Affective computing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg/220px-Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg/330px-Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg/440px-Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kismet_(robot)" title="Kismet (robot)">Kismet</a>, a robot with rudimentary social skills<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Kismet.27.27_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Kismet.27.27-80">[80]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Affective computing is the study and development of systems and devices that can recognize, interpret, process, and simulate human <a href="/wiki/Affect_(psychology)" title="Affect (psychology)">affects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThro1993_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThro1993-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdelson1991_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdelson1991-82">[82]</a></sup> It is an interdisciplinary field spanning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_sciences" title="Computer sciences">computer sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaoTan2005_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaoTan2005-83">[83]</a></sup> While the origins of the field may be traced as far back as to early philosophical inquiries into <a href="/wiki/Emotion#The_James-Lange_Theory" title="Emotion">emotion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1884_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1884-84">[84]</a></sup> the more modern branch of computer science originated with <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Picard" title="Rosalind Picard">Rosalind Picard</a>'s 1995 paper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicard1995_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicard1995-85">[85]</a></sup> on affective computing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> A motivation for the research is the ability to simulate <a href="/wiki/Empathy" title="Empathy">empathy</a>. The machine should interpret the emotional state of humans and adapt its behaviour to them, giving an appropriate response for those emotions.</p>
<p>Emotion and social skills<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emotion_and_affective_computing_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emotion_and_affective_computing-88">[88]</a></sup> play two roles for an intelligent agent. First, it must be able to predict the actions of others, by understanding their motives and emotional states. (This involves elements of <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, as well as the ability to model human emotions and the perceptual skills to detect emotions.) Also, in an effort to facilitate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human-computer_interaction" title="Human-computer interaction">human-computer interaction</a>, an intelligent machine might want to be able to <i>display</i> emotions—even if it does not actually experience them itself—in order to appear sensitive to the emotional dynamics of human interaction.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Creativity">Creativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Creativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Computational_creativity" title="Computational creativity">Computational creativity</a></div>
<p>A sub-field of AI addresses <a href="/wiki/Creativity" title="Creativity">creativity</a> both theoretically (from a philosophical and psychological perspective) and practically (via specific implementations of systems that generate outputs that can be considered creative, or systems that identify and assess creativity). Related areas of computational research are <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intuition" title="Artificial intuition">Artificial intuition</a> and Artificial thinking.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="General_intelligence">General intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: General intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">Artificial general intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/AI-complete" title="AI-complete">AI-complete</a></div>
<p>Many researchers think that their work will eventually be incorporated into a machine with <i>general</i> intelligence (known as <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">strong AI</a>), combining all the skills above and exceeding human abilities at most or all of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-General_intelligence_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-General_intelligence-8">[8]</a></sup> A few believe that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropomorphic" title="Anthropomorphic">anthropomorphic</a> features like <a href="/wiki/Artificial_consciousness" title="Artificial consciousness">artificial consciousness</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">artificial brain</a> may be required for such a project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Artificial_consciousness_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Artificial_consciousness-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brain_simulation_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brain_simulation-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many of the problems above may require general intelligence to be considered solved. For example, even a straightforward, specific task like <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">machine translation</a> requires that the machine read and write in both languages (<a href="#Natural_language_processing">NLP</a>), follow the author's argument (<a href="#Deduction.2C_reasoning.2C_problem_solving">reason</a>), know what is being talked about (<a href="#Knowledge_representation">knowledge</a>), and faithfully reproduce the author's intention (<a href="#Social_intelligence">social intelligence</a>). A problem like <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">machine translation</a> is considered "<a href="/wiki/AI-complete" title="AI-complete">AI-complete</a>". In order to solve this particular problem, one must solve all the problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_complete_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_complete-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches">Approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no established unifying theory or <a href="/wiki/Paradigm" title="Paradigm">paradigm</a> that guides AI research. Researchers disagree about many issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> A few of the most long standing questions that have remained unanswered are these: should artificial intelligence simulate natural intelligence by studying <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a>? Or is <a href="/wiki/Human_biology" title="Human biology">human biology</a> as irrelevant to AI research as bird biology is to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeronautical_engineering" title="Aeronautical engineering">aeronautical engineering</a>?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-93">[93]</a></sup> Can intelligent behavior be described using simple, elegant principles (such as <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" title="Optimization (mathematics)">optimization</a>)? Or does it necessarily require solving a large number of completely unrelated problems?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-94">[94]</a></sup> Can intelligence be reproduced using high-level symbols, similar to words and ideas? Or does it require "sub-symbolic" processing?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic-95">[95]</a></sup> John Haugeland, who coined the term GOFAI (Good Old-Fashioned Artificial Intelligence), also proposed that AI should more properly be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_intelligence" title="Synthetic intelligence">synthetic intelligence</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaugeland1985255_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaugeland1985255-96">[96]</a></sup> a term which has since been adopted by some non-GOFAI researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaw1994_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaw1994-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBach2008_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBach2008-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cybernetics_and_brain_simulation">Cybernetics and brain simulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cybernetics and brain simulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">Cybernetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computational_neuroscience" title="Computational neuroscience">Computational neuroscience</a></div>
<p>In the 1940s and 1950s, a number of researchers explored the connection between <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a>. Some of them built machines that used electronic networks to exhibit rudimentary intelligence, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._Grey_Walter" title="W. Grey Walter">W. Grey Walter</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Turtle_(robot)" title="Turtle (robot)">turtles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Beast" title="Johns Hopkins Beast">Johns Hopkins Beast</a>. Many of these researchers gathered for meetings of the Teleological Society at <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ratio_Club" title="Ratio Club">Ratio Club</a> in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI.27s_immediate_precursors_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-AI.27s_immediate_precursors-17">[17]</a></sup> By 1960, this approach was largely abandoned, although elements of it would be revived in the 1980s.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic">Symbolic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Symbolic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbolic_AI" title="Symbolic AI">Symbolic AI</a></div>
<p>When access to digital computers became possible in the middle 1950s, AI research began to explore the possibility that human intelligence could be reduced to symbol manipulation. The research was centered in three institutions: <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanford" title="Stanford">Stanford</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a>, and each one developed its own style of research. <a href="/wiki/John_Haugeland" title="John Haugeland">John Haugeland</a> named these approaches to AI "good old fashioned AI" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GOFAI" title="GOFAI">GOFAI</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOFAI_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-GOFAI-99">[99]</a></sup> During the 1960s, symbolic approaches had achieved great success at simulating high-level thinking in small demonstration programs. Approaches based on <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_network" title="Neural network">neural networks</a> were abandoned or pushed into the background.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Researchers in the 1960s and the 1970s were convinced that symbolic approaches would eventually succeed in creating a machine with <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">artificial general intelligence</a> and considered this the goal of their field.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Cognitive simulation</dt>
<dd>Economist <a href="/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon" title="Herbert A. Simon">Herbert Simon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allen_Newell" title="Allen Newell">Allen Newell</a> studied human problem-solving skills and attempted to formalize them, and their work laid the foundations of the field of artificial intelligence, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a> and <a href="/wiki/Management_science" title="Management science">management science</a>. Their research team used the results of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a> experiments to develop programs that simulated the techniques that people used to solve problems. This tradition, centered at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a> would eventually culminate in the development of the <a href="/wiki/Soar_(cognitive_architecture)" title="Soar (cognitive architecture)">Soar</a> architecture in the middle 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_at_CMU_in_the_60s_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_CMU_in_the_60s-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soar_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soar-102">[102]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Logic-based</dt>
<dd>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Allen_Newell" title="Allen Newell">Newell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon" title="Herbert A. Simon">Simon</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">John McCarthy</a> felt that machines did not need to simulate human thought, but should instead try to find the essence of abstract reasoning and problem solving, regardless of whether people used the same algorithms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-93">[93]</a></sup> His laboratory at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanford_Artificial_Intelligence_Laboratory" title="Stanford Artificial Intelligence Laboratory">SAIL</a>) focused on using formal <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> to solve a wide variety of problems, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation">knowledge representation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_at_Stanford_in_the_60s_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_Stanford_in_the_60s-103">[103]</a></sup> Logic was also the focus of the work at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> and elsewhere in Europe which led to the development of the programming language <a href="/wiki/Prolog" title="Prolog">Prolog</a> and the science of <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">logic programming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_at_Edinburgh_and_France_in_the_60s_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_Edinburgh_and_France_in_the_60s-104">[104]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>"Anti-logic" or "scruffy"</dt>
<dd>Researchers at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Papert" title="Seymour Papert">Seymour Papert</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_at_MIT_in_the_60s_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_MIT_in_the_60s-105">[105]</a></sup> found that solving difficult problems in <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">vision</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">natural language processing</a> required ad-hoc solutions – they argued that there was no simple and general principle (like <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>) that would capture all the aspects of intelligent behavior. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Schank" title="Roger Schank">Roger Schank</a> described their "anti-logic" approaches as "<a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" title="Neats vs. scruffies">scruffy</a>" (as opposed to the "<a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" title="Neats vs. scruffies">neat</a>" paradigms at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">CMU</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanford" title="Stanford">Stanford</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-94">[94]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge_bases" title="Commonsense knowledge bases">Commonsense knowledge bases</a> (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doug_Lenat" title="Doug Lenat">Doug Lenat</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Cyc" title="Cyc">Cyc</a>) are an example of "scruffy" AI, since they must be built by hand, one complicated concept at a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cyc_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cyc-106">[106]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Knowledge-based</dt>
<dd>When computers with large memories became available around 1970, researchers from all three traditions began to build <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation">knowledge</a> into AI applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knowledge_revolution_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_revolution-107">[107]</a></sup> This "knowledge revolution" led to the development and deployment of <a href="/wiki/Expert_system" title="Expert system">expert systems</a> (introduced by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Feigenbaum" title="Edward Feigenbaum">Edward Feigenbaum</a>), the first truly successful form of AI software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Expert_systems_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Expert_systems-27">[27]</a></sup> The knowledge revolution was also driven by the realization that enormous amounts of knowledge would be required by many simple AI applications.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-symbolic">Sub-symbolic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sub-symbolic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By the 1980s progress in symbolic AI seemed to stall and many believed that symbolic systems would never be able to imitate all the processes of human cognition, especially <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition" title="Pattern recognition">pattern recognition</a>. A number of researchers began to look into "sub-symbolic" approaches to specific AI problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic-95">[95]</a></sup> Sub-symbolic methods manage to approach intelligence without specific representations of knowledge.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Bottom-up, <a href="/wiki/Embodied_agent" title="Embodied agent">embodied</a>, <a href="/wiki/Situated" title="Situated">situated</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavior-based_AI" title="Behavior-based AI">behavior-based</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nouvelle_AI" title="Nouvelle AI">nouvelle AI</a></dt>
<dd>Researchers from the related field of <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Brooks" title="Rodney Brooks">Rodney Brooks</a>, rejected symbolic AI and focused on the basic engineering problems that would allow robots to move and survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Embodied_AI_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Embodied_AI-108">[108]</a></sup> Their work revived the non-symbolic viewpoint of the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cybernetic" title="Cybernetic">cybernetics</a> researchers of the 1950s and reintroduced the use of <a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">control theory</a> in AI. This coincided with the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embodied_mind_thesis" title="Embodied mind thesis">embodied mind thesis</a> in the related field of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>: the idea that aspects of the body (such as movement, perception and visualization) are required for higher intelligence.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Computational_intelligence" title="Computational intelligence">Computational intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soft_computing" title="Soft computing">soft computing</a></dt>
<dd>Interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_networks" title="Neural networks">neural networks</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">connectionism</a>" was revived by <a href="/wiki/David_Rumelhart" title="David Rumelhart">David Rumelhart</a> and others in the middle 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Revival_of_connectionism_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Revival_of_connectionism-109">[109]</a></sup> Neural networks are an example of <a href="/wiki/Soft_computing" title="Soft computing">soft computing</a> --- they are solutions to problems which cannot be solved with complete logical certainty, and where an approximate solution is often enough. Other soft computing approaches to AI include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuzzy_system" title="Fuzzy system">fuzzy systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">evolutionary computation</a> and many statistical tools. The application of soft computing to AI is studied collectively by the emerging discipline of <a href="/wiki/Computational_intelligence" title="Computational intelligence">computational intelligence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Computational_intelligence_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Computational_intelligence-110">[110]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistical">Statistical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Statistical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 1990s, AI researchers developed sophisticated mathematical tools to solve specific subproblems. These tools are truly <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific</a>, in the sense that their results are both measurable and verifiable, and they have been responsible for many of AI's recent successes. The shared mathematical language has also permitted a high level of collaboration with more established fields (like <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, economics or <a href="/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a>). <a href="/wiki/Stuart_J._Russell" title="Stuart J. Russell">Stuart Russell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Norvig" title="Peter Norvig">Peter Norvig</a> describe this movement as nothing less than a "revolution" and "the victory of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neats_and_scruffies" title="Neats and scruffies">neats</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Formal_methods_in_AI_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Formal_methods_in_AI-30">[30]</a></sup> Critics argue that these techniques (with few exceptions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutter2012_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutter2012-111">[111]</a></sup>) are too focused on particular problems and have failed to address the long-term goal of general intelligence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangley2011_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangley2011-112">[112]</a></sup> There is an ongoing debate about the relevance and validity of statistical approaches in AI, exemplified in part by exchanges between <a href="/wiki/Peter_Norvig" title="Peter Norvig">Peter Norvig</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKatz2012_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKatz2012-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorvig2012_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorvig2012-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrating_the_approaches">Integrating the approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Integrating the approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dt>Intelligent agent paradigm</dt>
<dd>An <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agent" title="Intelligent agent">intelligent agent</a> is a system that perceives its environment and takes actions which maximize its chances of success. The simplest intelligent agents are programs that solve specific problems. More complicated agents include human beings and organizations of human beings (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firm" title="Firm">firms</a>). The paradigm gives researchers license to study isolated problems and find solutions that are both verifiable and useful, without agreeing on one single approach. An agent that solves a specific problem can use any approach that works – some agents are symbolic and logical, some are sub-symbolic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_network" title="Neural network">neural networks</a> and others may use new approaches. The paradigm also gives researchers a common language to communicate with other fields—such as <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a> and economics—that also use concepts of abstract agents. The intelligent agent paradigm became widely accepted during the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intelligent_agents_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Intelligent_agents-1">[1]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Agent_architecture" title="Agent architecture">Agent architectures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_architecture" title="Cognitive architecture">cognitive architectures</a></dt>
<dd>Researchers have designed systems to build intelligent systems out of interacting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intelligent_agents" title="Intelligent agents">intelligent agents</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Multi-agent_system" title="Multi-agent system">multi-agent system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agent_architectures_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agent_architectures-115">[115]</a></sup> A system with both symbolic and sub-symbolic components is a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_intelligent_system" title="Hybrid intelligent system">hybrid intelligent system</a>, and the study of such systems is <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_systems_integration" title="Artificial intelligence systems integration">artificial intelligence systems integration</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_control_system" title="Hierarchical control system">hierarchical control system</a> provides a bridge between sub-symbolic AI at its lowest, reactive levels and traditional symbolic AI at its highest levels, where relaxed time constraints permit planning and world modelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hierarchical_control_system_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hierarchical_control_system-116">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Brooks" title="Rodney Brooks">Rodney Brooks</a>' <a href="/wiki/Subsumption_architecture" title="Subsumption architecture">subsumption architecture</a> was an early proposal for such a hierarchical system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subsumption_architecture_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Subsumption_architecture-117">[117]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tools">Tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the course of 50 years of research, AI has developed a large number of tools to solve the most difficult problems in <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>. A few of the most general of these methods are discussed below.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_and_optimization">Search and optimization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Search and optimization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Search_algorithm" title="Search algorithm">Search algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_optimization" title="Mathematical optimization">Mathematical optimization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">Evolutionary computation</a></div>
<p>Many problems in AI can be solved in theory by intelligently searching through many possible solutions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Search_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Search-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="#Deduction.2C_reasoning.2C_problem_solving">Reasoning</a> can be reduced to performing a search.
