For example, logical proof can be viewed as searching for a path that leads from <a href="/wiki/Premise" title="Premise">premises</a> to <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence">conclusions</a>, where each step is the application of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inference_rule" title="Inference rule">inference rule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Logic_as_search_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Logic_as_search-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">Planning</a> algorithms search through trees of goals and subgoals, attempting to find a path to a target goal, a process called <a href="/wiki/Means-ends_analysis" title="Means-ends analysis">means-ends analysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planning_as_search_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planning_as_search-120">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">Robotics</a> algorithms for moving limbs and grasping objects use <a href="/wiki/Local_search_(optimization)" title="Local search (optimization)">local searches</a> in <a href="/wiki/Configuration_space" title="Configuration space">configuration space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Configuration_space_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Configuration_space-77">[77]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> algorithms use search algorithms based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" title="Optimization (mathematics)">optimization</a>.</p>
<p>Simple exhaustive searches<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uninformed_search_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uninformed_search-121">[121]</a></sup> are rarely sufficient for most real world problems: the <a href="/wiki/Search_algorithm" title="Search algorithm">search space</a> (the number of places to search) quickly grows to <a href="/wiki/Large_numbers#Astronomically_large_numbers" title="Large numbers">astronomical numbers</a>. The result is a search that is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computation_time" title="Computation time">too slow</a> or never completes. The solution, for many problems, is to use "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heuristics" title="Heuristics">heuristics</a>" or "rules of thumb" that eliminate choices that are unlikely to lead to the goal (called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pruning_(algorithm)" title="Pruning (algorithm)">pruning</a> the <a href="/wiki/Search_tree" title="Search tree">search tree</a>"). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heuristics" title="Heuristics">Heuristics</a> supply the program with a "best guess" for the path on which the solution lies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Informed_search_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Informed_search-122">[122]</a></sup> Heuristics limit the search for solutions into a smaller sample size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETecuci2012_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETecuci2012-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>A very different kind of search came to prominence in the 1990s, based on the mathematical theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" title="Optimization (mathematics)">optimization</a>. For many problems, it is possible to begin the search with some form of a guess and then refine the guess incrementally until no more refinements can be made. These algorithms can be visualized as blind <a href="/wiki/Hill_climbing" title="Hill climbing">hill climbing</a>: we begin the search at a random point on the landscape, and then, by jumps or steps, we keep moving our guess uphill, until we reach the top. Other optimization algorithms are <a href="/wiki/Simulated_annealing" title="Simulated annealing">simulated annealing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beam_search" title="Beam search">beam search</a> and <a href="/wiki/Random_optimization" title="Random optimization">random optimization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Optimization_search_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Optimization_search-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">Evolutionary computation</a> uses a form of optimization search. For example, they may begin with a population of organisms (the guesses) and then allow them to mutate and recombine, <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">selecting</a> only the fittest to survive each generation (refining the guesses). Forms of <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">evolutionary computation</a> include <a href="/wiki/Swarm_intelligence" title="Swarm intelligence">swarm intelligence</a> algorithms (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ant_colony_optimization" title="Ant colony optimization">ant colony</a> or <a href="/wiki/Particle_swarm_optimization" title="Particle swarm optimization">particle swarm optimization</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Society_based_learning_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Society_based_learning-124">[124]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_algorithms" title="Evolutionary algorithms">evolutionary algorithms</a> (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_algorithms" title="Genetic algorithms">genetic algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression_programming" title="Gene expression programming">gene expression programming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_programming" title="Genetic programming">genetic programming</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetic_programming_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genetic_programming-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">Logic programming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Automated_reasoning" title="Automated reasoning">Automated reasoning</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Logic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Logic_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Logic-126">[126]</a></sup> is used for knowledge representation and problem solving, but it can be applied to other problems as well. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Satplan" title="Satplan">satplan</a> algorithm uses logic for <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Satplan_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Satplan-127">[127]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_logic_programming" title="Inductive logic programming">inductive logic programming</a> is a method for <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Symbolic_learning_techniques_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Symbolic_learning_techniques-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several different forms of logic are used in AI research. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propositional_logic" title="Propositional logic">Propositional</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sentential_logic" title="Sentential logic">sentential logic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Propositional_logic_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Propositional_logic-129">[129]</a></sup> is the logic of statements which can be true or false. <a href="/wiki/First-order_logic" title="First-order logic">First-order logic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-First-order_logic_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-First-order_logic-130">[130]</a></sup> also allows the use of <a href="/wiki/Quantifier_(logic)" title="Quantifier (logic)">quantifiers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(mathematical_logic)" title="Predicate (mathematical logic)">predicates</a>, and can express facts about objects, their properties, and their relations with each other. <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_logic" title="Fuzzy logic">Fuzzy logic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuzzy_logic_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fuzzy_logic-131">[131]</a></sup> is a version of first-order logic which allows the truth of a statement to be represented as a value between 0 and 1, rather than simply True (1) or False (0). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuzzy_system" title="Fuzzy system">Fuzzy systems</a> can be used for uncertain reasoning and have been widely used in modern industrial and consumer <a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition" title="Pattern recognition">product control systems</a>. <a href="/wiki/Subjective_logic" title="Subjective logic">Subjective logic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subjective_logic_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Subjective_logic-132">[132]</a></sup> models uncertainty in a different and more explicit manner than fuzzy-logic: a given binomial opinion satisfies belief + disbelief + uncertainty = 1 within a <a href="/wiki/Beta_distribution" title="Beta distribution">Beta distribution</a>. By this method, ignorance can be distinguished from probabilistic statements that an agent makes with high confidence.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Default_logic" title="Default logic">Default logics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-monotonic_logic" title="Non-monotonic logic">non-monotonic logics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circumscription_(logic)" title="Circumscription (logic)">circumscription</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic-50">[50]</a></sup> are forms of logic designed to help with default reasoning and the <a href="/wiki/Qualification_problem" title="Qualification problem">qualification problem</a>. Several extensions of logic have been designed to handle specific domains of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation">knowledge</a>, such as: <a href="/wiki/Description_logic" title="Description logic">description logics</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Representing_categories_and_relations_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_categories_and_relations-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Situation_calculus" title="Situation calculus">situation calculus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Event_calculus" title="Event calculus">event calculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluent_calculus" title="Fluent calculus">fluent calculus</a> (for representing events and time);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Representing_time_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_time-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Causality#Causal_calculus" title="Causality">causal calculus</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Representing_causation_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_causation-46">[46]</a></sup> belief calculus;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Probabilistic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning">Probabilistic methods for uncertain reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Probabilistic methods for uncertain reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_network" title="Bayesian network">Bayesian network</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hidden_Markov_model" title="Hidden Markov model">Hidden Markov model</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalman_filter" title="Kalman filter">Kalman filter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">Decision theory</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utility_theory" title="Utility theory">Utility theory</a></div>
<p>Many problems in AI (in reasoning, planning, learning, perception and robotics) require the agent to operate with incomplete or uncertain information. AI researchers have devised a number of powerful tools to solve these problems using methods from <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> theory and economics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stochastic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bayesian_network" title="Bayesian network">Bayesian networks</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayesian_networks_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_networks-135">[135]</a></sup> are a very general tool that can be used for a large number of problems: reasoning (using the <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian inference</a> algorithm),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayesian_inference_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_inference-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> (using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expectation-maximization_algorithm" title="Expectation-maximization algorithm">expectation-maximization algorithm</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayesian_learning_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_learning-137">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a> (using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decision_network" title="Decision network">decision networks</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayesian_decision_networks_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_decision_networks-138">[138]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a> (using <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_Bayesian_network" title="Dynamic Bayesian network">dynamic Bayesian networks</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-139">[139]</a></sup> Probabilistic algorithms can also be used for filtering, prediction, smoothing and finding explanations for streams of data, helping <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a> systems to analyze processes that occur over time (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Hidden_Markov_model" title="Hidden Markov model">hidden Markov models</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kalman_filter" title="Kalman filter">Kalman filters</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>A key concept from the science of economics is "<a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a>": a measure of how valuable something is to an intelligent agent. Precise mathematical tools have been developed that analyze how an agent can make choices and plan, using <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision_analysis" title="Decision analysis">decision analysis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Decisions_theory_and_analysis_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Decisions_theory_and_analysis-140">[140]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Applied_information_economics" title="Applied information economics">information value theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Information_value_theory_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Information_value_theory-56">[56]</a></sup> These tools include models such as <a href="/wiki/Markov_decision_process" title="Markov decision process">Markov decision processes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Markov_decision_process_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Markov_decision_process-141">[141]</a></sup> dynamic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decision_network" title="Decision network">decision networks</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_design" title="Mechanism design">mechanism design</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Game_theory_and_mechanism_design_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Game_theory_and_mechanism_design-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifiers_and_statistical_learning_methods">Classifiers and statistical learning methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Classifiers and statistical learning methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classifier_(mathematics)" title="Classifier (mathematics)">Classifier (mathematics)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statistical_classification" title="Statistical classification">Statistical classification</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">Machine learning</a></div>
<p>The simplest AI applications can be divided into two types: classifiers ("if shiny then diamond") and controllers ("if shiny then pick up"). Controllers do, however, also classify conditions before inferring actions, and therefore classification forms a central part of many AI systems. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classifier_(mathematics)" title="Classifier (mathematics)">Classifiers</a> are functions that use <a href="/wiki/Pattern_matching" title="Pattern matching">pattern matching</a> to determine a closest match. They can be tuned according to examples, making them very attractive for use in AI. These examples are known as observations or patterns. In supervised learning, each pattern belongs to a certain predefined class. A class can be seen as a decision that has to be made. All the observations combined with their class labels are known as a data set. When a new observation is received, that observation is classified based on previous experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Classifiers_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Classifiers-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>A classifier can be trained in various ways; there are many statistical and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> approaches. The most widely used classifiers are the <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">neural network</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neural_networks_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neural_networks-144">[144]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_methods" title="Kernel methods">kernel methods</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Support_vector_machine" title="Support vector machine">support vector machine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kernel_methods_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kernel_methods-145">[145]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm" title="K-nearest neighbor algorithm">k-nearest neighbor algorithm</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm-146">[146]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaussian_mixture_model" title="Gaussian mixture model">Gaussian mixture model</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaussian_mixture_model_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaussian_mixture_model-147">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Naive_Bayes_classifier" title="Naive Bayes classifier">naive Bayes classifier</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naive_Bayes_classifier_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naive_Bayes_classifier-148">[148]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Decision_tree_learning" title="Decision tree learning">decision tree</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Decision_tree_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Decision_tree-149">[149]</a></sup> The performance of these classifiers have been compared over a wide range of tasks. Classifier performance depends greatly on the characteristics of the data to be classified. There is no single classifier that works best on all given problems; this is also referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/No_free_lunch_in_search_and_optimization" title="No free lunch in search and optimization">no free lunch</a>" theorem. Determining a suitable classifier for a given problem is still more an art than science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Classifier_performance_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Classifier_performance-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neural_networks">Neural networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Neural networks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">Artificial neural network</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">Connectionism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artificial_neural_network.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="560" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Artificial_neural_network.svg/220px-Artificial_neural_network.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Artificial_neural_network.svg/330px-Artificial_neural_network.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Artificial_neural_network.svg/440px-Artificial_neural_network.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artificial_neural_network.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A neural network is an interconnected group of nodes, akin to the vast network of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The study of non-learning <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural networks</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neural_networks_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neural_networks-144">[144]</a></sup> began in the decade before the field of AI research was founded, in the work of <a href="/wiki/Walter_Pitts" title="Walter Pitts">Walter Pitts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warren_McCullough" title="Warren McCullough">Warren McCullough</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Rosenblatt" title="Frank Rosenblatt">Frank Rosenblatt</a> invented the <a href="/wiki/Perceptron" title="Perceptron">perceptron</a>, a learning network with a single layer, similar to the old concept of <a href="/wiki/Linear_regression" title="Linear regression">linear regression</a>. Early pioneers also include <a href="/wiki/Alexey_Grigorevich_Ivakhnenko" title="Alexey Grigorevich Ivakhnenko">Alexey Grigorevich Ivakhnenko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teuvo_Kohonen" title="Teuvo Kohonen">Teuvo Kohonen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Grossberg" title="Stephen Grossberg">Stephen Grossberg</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kunihiko_Fukushima" title="Kunihiko Fukushima">Kunihiko Fukushima</a>, Christoph von der Malsburg, David Willshaw, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shun-Ichi_Amari" title="Shun-Ichi Amari">Shun-Ichi Amari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Widrow" title="Bernard Widrow">Bernard Widrow</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Hopfield" title="John Hopfield">John Hopfield</a>, and others.</p>
<p>The main categories of networks are acyclic or <a href="/wiki/Feedforward_neural_network" title="Feedforward neural network">feedforward neural networks</a> (where the signal passes in only one direction) and <a href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_network" title="Recurrent neural network">recurrent neural networks</a> (which allow feedback and short-term memories of previous input events). Among the most popular feedforward networks are <a href="/wiki/Perceptron" title="Perceptron">perceptrons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-layer_perceptron" title="Multi-layer perceptron">multi-layer perceptrons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial_basis_network" title="Radial basis network">radial basis networks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feedforward_neural_networks_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feedforward_neural_networks-151">[151]</a></sup> Neural networks can be applied to the problem of <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_control" title="Intelligent control">intelligent control</a> (for robotics) or <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>, using such techniques as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebbian_learning" title="Hebbian learning">Hebbian learning</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GMDH" title="GMDH">GMDH</a> or <a href="/wiki/Competitive_learning" title="Competitive learning">competitive learning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Learning_in_neural_networks_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Learning_in_neural_networks-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today, neural networks are often trained by the <a href="/wiki/Backpropagation" title="Backpropagation">backpropagation</a> algorithm, which had been around since 1970 as the reverse mode of <a href="/wiki/Automatic_differentiation" title="Automatic differentiation">automatic differentiation</a> published by <a href="/wiki/Seppo_Linnainmaa" title="Seppo Linnainmaa">Seppo Linnainmaa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lin1970_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-lin1970-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grie2012_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-grie2012-154">[154]</a></sup> and was introduced to neural networks by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Werbos" title="Paul Werbos">Paul Werbos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WERBOS1974_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-WERBOS1974-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-werbos1982_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-werbos1982-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backpropagation_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backpropagation-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_temporal_memory" title="Hierarchical temporal memory">Hierarchical temporal memory</a> is an approach that models some of the structural and algorithmic properties of the <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hierarchical_temporal_memory_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hierarchical_temporal_memory-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deep_feedforward_neural_networks">Deep feedforward neural networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Deep feedforward neural networks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deep_learning" title="Deep learning">Deep learning</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deep_learning" title="Deep learning">Deep learning</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_networks" title="Artificial neural networks">artificial neural networks</a> with many layers has transformed many important subfields of artificial intelligence, including <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">computer vision</a>, <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">speech recognition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">natural language processing</a> and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodfellow2016_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-goodfellow2016-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HintonDengYu2012_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-HintonDengYu2012-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmidhuber2015_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-schmidhuber2015-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a survey,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scholarpedia_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-scholarpedia-162">[162]</a></sup> the expression "Deep Learning" was introduced to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machine_Learning" title="Machine Learning">Machine Learning</a> community by <a href="/wiki/Rina_Dechter" title="Rina Dechter">Rina Dechter</a> in 1986<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dechter1986_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-dechter1986-163">[163]</a></sup> and gained traction after Igor Aizenberg and colleagues introduced it to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_Neural_Networks" title="Artificial Neural Networks">Artificial Neural Networks</a> in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aizenberg2000_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-aizenberg2000-164">[164]</a></sup> The first functional Deep Learning networks were published by <a href="/wiki/Alexey_Grigorevich_Ivakhnenko" title="Alexey Grigorevich Ivakhnenko">Alexey Grigorevich Ivakhnenko</a> and V. G. Lapa in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ivak1965_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-ivak1965-165">[165]</a></sup> These networks are trained one layer at a time. Ivakhnenko's 1971 paper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ivak1971_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-ivak1971-166">[166]</a></sup> describes the learning of a deep feedforward multilayer perceptron with eight layers, already much deeper than many later networks. In 2006, a publication by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton" title="Geoffrey Hinton">Geoffrey Hinton</a> and Ruslan Salakhutdinov introduced another way of pre-training many-layered <a href="/wiki/Feedforward_neural_network" title="Feedforward neural network">feedforward neural networks</a> (FNNs) one layer at a time, treating each layer in turn as an <a href="/wiki/Unsupervised_learning" title="Unsupervised learning">unsupervised</a> <a href="/wiki/Restricted_Boltzmann_machine" title="Restricted Boltzmann machine">restricted Boltzmann machine</a>, then using <a href="/wiki/Supervised_learning" title="Supervised learning">supervised</a> <a href="/wiki/Backpropagation" title="Backpropagation">backpropagation</a> for fine-tuning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinton2007_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinton2007-167">[167]</a></sup> Similar to shallow artificial neural networks, deep neural networks can model complex non-linear relationships. Over the last few years, advances in both machine learning algorithms and computer hardware have led to more efficient methods for training deep neural networks that contain many layers of non-linear hidden units and a very large output layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Deep learning often uses <a href="/wiki/Convolutional_neural_network" title="Convolutional neural network">convolutional neural networks</a> (CNNs), whose origins can be traced back to the <a href="/wiki/Neocognitron" title="Neocognitron">Neocognitron</a> introduced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kunihiko_Fukushima" title="Kunihiko Fukushima">Kunihiko Fukushima</a> in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FUKU1980_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FUKU1980-169">[169]</a></sup> In 1989, <a href="/wiki/Yann_LeCun" title="Yann LeCun">Yann LeCun</a> and colleagues applied <a href="/wiki/Backpropagation" title="Backpropagation">backpropagation</a> to such an architecture. In the early 2000s, in an industrial application CNNs already processed an estimated 10% to 20% of all the checks written in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lecun2016slides_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-lecun2016slides-170">[170]</a></sup> Since 2011, fast implementations of CNNs on GPUs have won many visual pattern recognition competitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmidhuber2015_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-schmidhuber2015-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>Deep feedforward neural networks were used in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Reinforcement_learning" title="Reinforcement learning">reinforcement learning</a> by <a href="/wiki/AlphaGo" title="AlphaGo">AlphaGo</a>, Google Deepmind's program that was the first to beat a professional human player.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deep_recurrent_neural_networks">Deep recurrent neural networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Deep recurrent neural networks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_networks" title="Recurrent neural networks">Recurrent neural networks</a></div>
<p>Early on, <a href="/wiki/Deep_learning" title="Deep learning">deep learning</a> was also applied to sequence learning with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_networks" title="Recurrent neural networks">recurrent neural networks</a> (RNNs)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Recurrent_neural_networks_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Recurrent_neural_networks-172">[172]</a></sup> which are general computers and can run arbitrary programs to process arbitrary sequences of inputs. The depth of an RNN is unlimited and depends on the length of its input sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmidhuber2015_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-schmidhuber2015-161">[161]</a></sup> RNNs can be trained by <a href="/wiki/Gradient_descent" title="Gradient descent">gradient descent</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> but suffer from the <a href="/wiki/Vanishing_gradient_problem" title="Vanishing gradient problem">vanishing gradient problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodfellow2016_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-goodfellow2016-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hochreiter1991_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-hochreiter1991-176">[176]</a></sup> In 1992, it was shown that unsupervised pre-training of a stack of <a href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_network" title="Recurrent neural network">recurrent neural networks</a> can speed up subsequent supervised learning of deep sequential problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCHMID1992_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCHMID1992-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>Numerous researchers now use variants of a deep learning recurrent NN called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_short_term_memory" title="Long short term memory">Long short term memory</a> (LSTM) network published by Hochreiter &amp; Schmidhuber in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lstm_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-lstm-178">[178]</a></sup> LSTM is often trained by Connectionist Temporal Classification (CTC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graves2006_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-graves2006-179">[179]</a></sup> At Google, Microsoft and Baidu this approach has revolutionised <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">speech recognition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hannun2014_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-hannun2014-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sak2014_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-sak2014-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liwu2015_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-liwu2015-182">[182]</a></sup> For example, in 2015, Google's speech recognition experienced a dramatic performance jump of 49% through CTC-trained LSTM, which is now available through <a href="/wiki/Google_Voice" title="Google Voice">Google Voice</a> to billions of smartphone users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sak2015_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-sak2015-183">[183]</a></sup> Google also used LSTM to improve machine translation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sutskever2014_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-sutskever2014-184">[184]</a></sup> Language Modeling<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vinyals2016_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-vinyals2016-185">[185]</a></sup> and Multilingual Language Processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gillick2015_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-gillick2015-186">[186]</a></sup> LSTM combined with CNNs also improved automatic image captioning<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vinyals2015_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-vinyals2015-187">[187]</a></sup> and a plethora of other applications.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Control_theory">Control theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Control theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_control" title="Intelligent control">Intelligent control</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">Control theory</a>, the grandchild of <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a>, has many important applications, especially in <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Control_theory_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Control_theory-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_programming_languages_for_artificial_intelligence" title="List of programming languages for artificial intelligence">List of programming languages for artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>AI researchers have developed several specialized languages for AI research, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lisp_programming_language" title="Lisp programming language">Lisp</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lisp_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lisp-189">[189]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Prolog" title="Prolog">Prolog</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prolog_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prolog-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evaluating_progress">Evaluating progress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Evaluating progress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Progress_in_artificial_intelligence" title="Progress in artificial intelligence">Progress in artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>In 1950, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> proposed a general procedure to test the intelligence of an agent now known as the <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a>. This procedure allows almost all the major problems of artificial intelligence to be tested. However, it is a very difficult challenge and at present all agents fail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turing_test_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turing_test-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Artificial intelligence can also be evaluated on specific problems such as small problems in chemistry, hand-writing recognition and game-playing. Such tests have been termed <a href="/wiki/Subject_matter_expert_Turing_test" title="Subject matter expert Turing test">subject matter expert Turing tests</a>. Smaller problems provide more achievable goals and there are an ever-increasing number of positive results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subject_matter_expert_Turing_test_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Subject_matter_expert_Turing_test-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>One classification for outcomes of an AI test is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERajani2011_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERajani2011-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>Optimal: it is not possible to perform better.</li>
<li>Strong super-human: performs better than all humans.</li>
<li>Super-human: performs better than most humans.</li>
<li>Sub-human: performs worse than most humans.</li>
</ol>
<p>For example, performance at <a href="/wiki/Draughts" title="Draughts">draughts</a> (i.e. checkers) is optimal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Game_AI_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Game_AI-194">[194]</a></sup> performance at chess is super-human and nearing strong super-human (see <a href="/wiki/Computer_chess#Computers_versus_humans" title="Computer chess">computer chess: computers versus human</a>) and performance at many everyday tasks (such as recognizing a face or crossing a room without bumping into something) is sub-human.</p>
<p>A quite different approach measures machine intelligence through tests which are developed from <i>mathematical</i> definitions of intelligence. Examples of these kinds of tests start in the late nineties devising intelligence tests using notions from <a href="/wiki/Kolmogorov_complexity" title="Kolmogorov complexity">Kolmogorov complexity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Data_compression" title="Data compression">data compression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mathematical_definitions_of_intelligence_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mathematical_definitions_of_intelligence-195">[195]</a></sup> Two major advantages of mathematical definitions are their applicability to nonhuman intelligences and their absence of a requirement for human testers.</p>
<p>A derivative of the Turing test is the Completely Automated Public Turing test to tell Computers and Humans Apart (<a href="/wiki/CAPTCHA" title="CAPTCHA">CAPTCHA</a>). As the name implies, this helps to determine that a user is an actual person and not a computer posing as a human. In contrast to the standard Turing test, CAPTCHA administered by a machine and targeted to a human as opposed to being administered by a human and targeted to a machine. A computer asks a user to complete a simple test then generates a grade for that test. Computers are unable to solve the problem, so correct solutions are deemed to be the result of a person taking the test. A common type of CAPTCHA is the test that requires the typing of distorted letters, numbers or symbols that appear in an image undecipherable by a computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27BrienMarakas2011_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27BrienMarakas2011-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Automated_online_assistant.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="400" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Automated_online_assistant.png/220px-Automated_online_assistant.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Automated_online_assistant.png/330px-Automated_online_assistant.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8b/Automated_online_assistant.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Automated_online_assistant.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Automated_online_assistant" title="Automated online assistant">automated online assistant</a> providing customer service on a web page – one of many very primitive applications of artificial intelligence.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Applications_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Applications of artificial intelligence">Applications of artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>AI is relevant to any intellectual task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20091_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20091-197">[197]</a></sup> Modern artificial intelligence techniques are pervasive and are too numerous to list here. Frequently, when a technique reaches mainstream use, it is no longer considered artificial intelligence; this phenomenon is described as the <a href="/wiki/AI_effect" title="AI effect">AI effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27CNN.27.272006_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27CNN.27.272006-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>High-profile examples of AI include autonomous vehicles (such as <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">drones</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-driving_cars" title="Self-driving cars">self-driving cars</a>), medical diagnosis, creating art (such as poetry), proving mathemetical theorems, playing games (such as Chess or Go), search engines (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_search" title="Google search">Google search</a>), online assistants (such as <a href="/wiki/Siri" title="Siri">Siri</a>), image recognition in photographs, spam filtering, and targeting online advertisements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20091_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20091-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>With social media sites overtaking TV as a source for news for young people and news organisations increasingly reliant on social media platforms for generating distribution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> major publishers now use artificial intelligence (AI) technology to post stories more effectively and generate higher volumes of traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competitions_and_prizes">Competitions and prizes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Competitions and prizes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Competitions_and_prizes_in_artificial_intelligence" title="Competitions and prizes in artificial intelligence">Competitions and prizes in artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>There are a number of competitions and prizes to promote research in artificial intelligence. The main areas promoted are: general machine intelligence, conversational behavior, data-mining, <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_car" title="Autonomous car">robotic cars</a>, robot soccer and games.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Platforms">Platforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Platforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platform_(computing)" title="Platform (computing)">platform</a> (or "<a href="/wiki/Computing_platform" title="Computing platform">computing platform</a>") is defined as "some sort of hardware architecture or software framework (including application frameworks), that allows software to run." As Rodney Brooks pointed out many years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1991_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1991-203">[203]</a></sup> it is not just the artificial intelligence software that defines the AI features of the platform, but rather the actual platform itself that affects the AI that results, i.e., there needs to be work in AI problems on real-world platforms rather than in isolation.</p>
<p>A wide variety of platforms has allowed different aspects of AI to develop, ranging from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expert_systems" title="Expert systems">expert systems</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cyc" title="Cyc">Cyc</a> to <a href="/wiki/Deep_learning#Software_libraries" title="Deep learning">deep-learning frameworks</a> to robot platforms such as the <a href="/wiki/Roomba" title="Roomba">Roomba</a> with open interface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> Recent advances in deep <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural networks</a> and distributed computing have led to a proliferation of software libraries, including <a href="/wiki/Deeplearning4j" title="Deeplearning4j">Deeplearning4j</a>, <a href="/wiki/TensorFlow" title="TensorFlow">TensorFlow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theano_(software)" title="Theano (software)">Theano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torch_(machine_learning)" title="Torch (machine learning)">Torch</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_and_ethics">Philosophy and ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Philosophy and ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Philosophy of artificial intelligence">Philosophy of artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Ethics of artificial intelligence">Ethics of artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>There are three philosophical questions related to AI:</p>
<ol>
<li>Is <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">artificial general intelligence</a> possible? Can a machine solve any problem that a human being can solve using intelligence? Or are there hard limits to what a machine can accomplish?</li>
<li>Are intelligent machines dangerous? How can we ensure that machines behave ethically and that they are used ethically?</li>
<li>Can a machine have a <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">mental states</a> in exactly the same sense that human beings do? Can a machine be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sentient" title="Sentient">sentient</a>, and thus deserve certain rights? Can a machine <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intentionally</a> cause harm?</li>
</ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_limits_of_artificial_general_intelligence">The limits of artificial general intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: The limits of artificial general intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_AI" title="Philosophy of AI">philosophy of AI</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_symbol_systems_hypothesis" title="Physical symbol systems hypothesis">Physical symbol systems hypothesis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreyfus%27_critique_of_AI" title="Dreyfus' critique of AI">Dreyfus' critique of AI</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_New_Mind" title="The Emperor's New Mind">The Emperor's New Mind</a>, and <a href="/wiki/AI_effect" title="AI effect">AI effect</a></div>
<p>Can a machine be intelligent? Can it "think"?</p>
<dl>
<dt><i><a href="/wiki/Computing_Machinery_and_Intelligence" title="Computing Machinery and Intelligence">Turing's "polite convention"</a></i></dt>
<dd>We need not decide if a machine can "think"; we need only decide if a machine can act as intelligently as a human being. This approach to the philosophical problems associated with artificial intelligence forms the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turing_test_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-Turing_test-191">[191]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><i>The <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Conferences" title="Dartmouth Conferences">Dartmouth proposal</a></i></dt>
<dd>"Every aspect of learning or any other feature of intelligence can be so precisely described that a machine can be made to simulate it." This conjecture was printed in the proposal for the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Conferences" title="Dartmouth Conferences">Dartmouth Conference</a> of 1956, and represents the position of most working AI researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_proposal_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_proposal-205">[205]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><i><a href="/wiki/Physical_symbol_system" title="Physical symbol system">Newell and Simon's physical symbol system hypothesis</a></i></dt>
<dd>"A physical symbol system has the necessary and sufficient means of general intelligent action." Newell and Simon argue that intelligence consists of formal operations on symbols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physical_symbol_system_hypothesis_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Physical_symbol_system_hypothesis-206">[206]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus" title="Hubert Dreyfus">Hubert Dreyfus</a> argued that, on the contrary, human expertise depends on unconscious instinct rather than conscious symbol manipulation and on having a "feel" for the situation rather than explicit symbolic knowledge. (See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreyfus%27_critique_of_AI" title="Dreyfus' critique of AI">Dreyfus' critique of AI</a>.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dreyfus.27_critique_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dreyfus.27_critique-208">[208]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><i>Gödelian arguments</i></dt>
<dd><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del" title="Gödel">Gödel</a> himself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G.C3.B6del_himself_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-G.C3.B6del_himself-209">[209]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Lucas_(philosopher)" title="John Lucas (philosopher)">John Lucas</a> (in 1961) and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose" title="Roger Penrose">Roger Penrose</a> (in a more detailed argument from 1989 onwards) made highly technical arguments that human mathematicians can consistently see the truth of their own "Gödel statements" and therefore have computational abilities beyond that of mechanical Turing machines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_mathematical_objection_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_mathematical_objection-210">[210]</a></sup> However, the modern consensus in the scientific and mathematical community is that these "Gödelian arguments" fail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><i>The <a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">artificial brain</a> argument</i></dt>
<dd>The brain can be simulated by machines and because brains are intelligent, simulated brains must also be intelligent; thus machines can be intelligent. <a href="/wiki/Hans_Moravec" title="Hans Moravec">Hans Moravec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> and others have argued that it is technologically feasible to copy the brain directly into hardware and software, and that such a simulation will be essentially identical to the original.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brain_simulation_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brain_simulation-90">[90]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><i>The <a href="/wiki/AI_effect" title="AI effect">AI effect</a></i></dt>
<dd>Machines are <i>already</i> intelligent, but observers have failed to recognize it. When <a href="/wiki/Deep_Blue_(chess_computer)" title="Deep Blue (chess computer)">Deep Blue</a> beat <a href="/wiki/Garry_Kasparov" title="Garry Kasparov">Garry Kasparov</a> in chess, the machine was acting intelligently. However, onlookers commonly discount the behavior of an artificial intelligence program by arguing that it is not "real" intelligence after all; thus "real" intelligence is whatever intelligent behavior people can do that machines still can not. This is known as the AI Effect: "AI is whatever hasn't been done yet."</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intelligent_behaviour_and_machine_ethics">Intelligent behaviour and machine ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Intelligent behaviour and machine ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a minimum, an AI system must be able to reproduce aspects of human intelligence. This raises the issue of how ethically the machine should behave towards both humans and other AI agents. This issue was addressed by Wendell Wallach in his book titled <i>Moral Machines</i> in which he introduced the concept of <a href="/wiki/Moral_agency#Artificial_moral_agents" title="Moral agency">artificial moral agents</a> (AMA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> For Wallach, AMAs have become a part of the research landscape of artificial intelligence as guided by its two central questions which he identifies as "Does Humanity Want Computers Making Moral Decisions"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> and "Can (Ro)bots Really Be Moral".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> For Wallach the question is not centered on the issue of <i>whether</i> machines can demonstrate the equivalent of moral behavior in contrast to the <i>constraints</i> which society may place on the development of AMAs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Machine_ethics">Machine ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Machine ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Machine_ethics" title="Machine ethics">Machine ethics</a></div>
<p>The field of machine ethics is concerned with giving machines ethical principles, or a procedure for discovering a way to resolve the ethical dilemmas they might encounter, enabling them to function in an ethically responsible manner through their own ethical decision making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-218">[218]</a></sup> The field was delineated in the AAAI Fall 2005 Symposium on Machine Ethics: "Past research concerning the relationship between technology and ethics has largely focused on responsible and irresponsible use of technology by human beings, with a few people being interested in how human beings ought to treat machines. In all cases, only human beings have engaged in ethical reasoning. The time has come for adding an ethical dimension to at least some machines. Recognition of the ethical ramifications of behavior involving machines, as well as recent and potential developments in machine autonomy, necessitate this. In contrast to computer hacking, software property issues, privacy issues and other topics normally ascribed to computer ethics, machine ethics is concerned with the behavior of machines towards human users and other machines. Research in machine ethics is key to alleviating concerns with autonomous systems—it could be argued that the notion of autonomous machines without such a dimension is at the root of all fear concerning machine intelligence. Further, investigation of machine ethics could enable the discovery of problems with current ethical theories, advancing our thinking about Ethics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-219">[219]</a></sup> Machine ethics is sometimes referred to as machine morality, computational ethics or computational morality. A variety of perspectives of this nascent field can be found in the collected edition "Machine Ethics"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-218">[218]</a></sup> that stems from the AAAI Fall 2005 Symposium on Machine Ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-219">[219]</a></sup> Some suggest that to ensure that AI-equipped machines (sometimes called "smart machines") will act ethically requires a new kind of AI. This AI would be able to monitor, supervise, and if need be, correct the first order AI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malevolent_and_friendly_AI">Malevolent and friendly AI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Malevolent and friendly AI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friendly_AI" title="Friendly AI">Friendly AI</a></div>
<p>Political scientist <a href="/wiki/Charles_T._Rubin" title="Charles T. Rubin">Charles T. Rubin</a> believes that AI can be neither designed nor guaranteed to be benevolent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> He argues that "any sufficiently advanced benevolence may be indistinguishable from malevolence." Humans should not assume machines or robots would treat us favorably, because there is no <i>a priori</i> reason to believe that they would be sympathetic to our system of morality, which has evolved along with our particular biology (which AIs would not share). Hyper-intelligent software may not necessarily decide to support the continued existence of mankind, and would be extremely difficult to stop. This topic has also recently begun to be discussed in academic publications as a real source of risks to civilization, humans, and planet Earth.</p>
<p>Physicist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a> and <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> have expressed concerns about the possibility that AI could evolve to the point that humans could not control it, with Hawking theorizing that this could "<a href="/wiki/Global_catastrophic_risk" title="Global catastrophic risk">spell the end of the human race</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>One proposal to deal with this is to ensure that the first generally intelligent AI is '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friendly_AI" title="Friendly AI">Friendly AI</a>', and will then be able to control subsequently developed AIs. Some question whether this kind of check could really remain in place.</p>
<p>Leading AI researcher <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Brooks" title="Rodney Brooks">Rodney Brooks</a> writes, "I think it is a mistake to be worrying about us developing malevolent AI anytime in the next few hundred years. I think the worry stems from a fundamental error in not distinguishing the difference between the very real recent advances in a particular aspect of AI, and the enormity and complexity of building sentient volitional intelligence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Devaluation_of_humanity">Devaluation of humanity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Devaluation of humanity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Computer_Power_and_Human_Reason" title="Computer Power and Human Reason">Computer Power and Human Reason</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum" title="Joseph Weizenbaum">Joseph Weizenbaum</a> wrote that AI applications can not, by definition, successfully simulate genuine human empathy and that the use of AI technology in fields such as <a href="/wiki/Customer_service" title="Customer service">customer service</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> was deeply misguided. Weizenbaum was also bothered that AI researchers (and some philosophers) were willing to view the human mind as nothing more than a computer program (a position now known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computationalism" title="Computationalism">computationalism</a>). To Weizenbaum these points suggest that AI research devalues human life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weizenbaum.27s_critique_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weizenbaum.27s_critique-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decrease_in_demand_for_human_labor">Decrease in demand for human labor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Decrease in demand for human labor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Martin Ford, author of <i>The Lights in the Tunnel: Automation, Accelerating Technology and the Economy of the Future</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFord2009The_lights_in_the_tunnel_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFord2009The_lights_in_the_tunnel-226">[226]</a></sup> and others argue that specialized artificial intelligence applications, robotics and other forms of automation will ultimately result in significant unemployment as machines begin to match and exceed the capability of workers to perform most routine and repetitive jobs. Ford predicts that many knowledge-based occupations—and in particular entry level jobs—will be increasingly susceptible to automation via expert systems, machine learning<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> and other AI-enhanced applications. AI-based applications may also be used to amplify the capabilities of low-wage offshore workers, making it more feasible to <a href="/wiki/Outsourcing" title="Outsourcing">outsource</a> <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_worker" title="Knowledge worker">knowledge work</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Replaced_by_machines_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Replaced_by_machines-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Machine_consciousness.2C_sentience_and_mind">Machine consciousness, sentience and mind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Machine consciousness, sentience and mind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_consciousness" title="Artificial consciousness">Artificial consciousness</a></div>
<p>If an AI system replicates all key aspects of human intelligence, will that system also be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sentient" title="Sentient">sentient</a> – will it have a <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> which has <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">conscious experiences</a>? This question is closely related to the philosophical problem as to the nature of human consciousness, generally referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Hard_problem_of_consciousness" title="Hard problem of consciousness">hard problem of consciousness</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consciousness">Consciousness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Consciousness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hard_problem_of_consciousness" title="Hard problem of consciousness">Hard problem of consciousness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_mind" title="Theory of mind">Theory of mind</a></div>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
