</tr>
</table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Computationalism">Computationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Computationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computationalism" title="Computationalism">Computationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Functionalism (philosophy of mind)">Functionalism (philosophy of mind)</a></div>
<p>Computationalism is the position in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a> that the human mind or the human brain (or both) is an information processing system and that thinking is a form of computing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Computationalism argues that the relationship between mind and body is similar or identical to the relationship between software and hardware and thus may be a solution to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind-body_problem" title="Mind-body problem">mind-body problem</a>. This philosophical position was inspired by the work of AI researchers and cognitive scientists in the 1960s and was originally proposed by philosophers <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Fodor" title="Jerry Fodor">Jerry Fodor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Putnam" title="Hilary Putnam">Hilary Putnam</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_AI_hypothesis">Strong AI hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Strong AI hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_room" title="Chinese room">Chinese room</a></div>
<p>The philosophical position that John Searle has named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_AI_hypothesis" title="Strong AI hypothesis">"strong AI"</a> states: "The appropriately programmed computer with the right inputs and outputs would thereby have a mind in exactly the same sense human beings have minds."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searle.27s_strong_AI_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Searle.27s_strong_AI-230">[230]</a></sup> Searle counters this assertion with his <a href="/wiki/Chinese_room" title="Chinese room">Chinese room</a> argument, which asks us to look <i>inside</i> the computer and try to find where the "mind" might be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chinese_room_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chinese_room-231">[231]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Robot_rights">Robot rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Robot rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robot_rights" title="Robot rights">Robot rights</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i> considers a key issue in the <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Ethics of artificial intelligence">ethics of artificial intelligence</a>: if a machine can be created that has intelligence, could it also <i><a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">feel</a></i>? If it can feel, does it have the same rights as a human? The idea also appears in modern science fiction, such as the film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.I.:_Artificial_Intelligence" title="A.I.: Artificial Intelligence">A.I.: Artificial Intelligence</a></i>, in which humanoid machines have the ability to feel emotions. This issue, now known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robot_rights" title="Robot rights">robot rights</a>", is currently being considered by, for example, California's <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_the_Future" title="Institute for the Future">Institute for the Future</a>, although many critics believe that the discussion is premature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robot_rights_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robot_rights-232">[232]</a></sup> The subject is profoundly discussed in the 2010 documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Plug_%26_Pray" title="Plug &amp; Pray">Plug &amp; Pray</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Superintelligence">Superintelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Superintelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Superintelligence" title="Superintelligence">Superintelligence</a></div>
<p>Are there limits to how intelligent machines – or human-machine hybrids – can be? A superintelligence, hyperintelligence, or superhuman intelligence is a hypothetical agent that would possess intelligence far surpassing that of the brightest and most gifted human mind. ‘’Superintelligence’’ may also refer to the form or degree of intelligence possessed by such an agent.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Technological_singularity">Technological singularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Technological singularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">Technological singularity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a></div>
<p>If research into <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">Strong AI</a> produced sufficiently intelligent software, it might be able to reprogram and improve itself. The improved software would be even better at improving itself, leading to <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_explosion" title="Intelligence explosion">recursive self-improvement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-recurse_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-recurse-234">[234]</a></sup> The new intelligence could thus increase exponentially and dramatically surpass humans. Science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">Vernor Vinge</a> named this scenario "<a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">singularity</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singularity_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singularity-235">[235]</a></sup> Technological singularity is when accelerating progress in technologies will cause a runaway effect wherein artificial intelligence will exceed human intellectual capacity and control, thus radically changing or even ending civilization. Because the capabilities of such an intelligence may be impossible to comprehend, the technological singularity is an occurrence beyond which events are unpredictable or even unfathomable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singularity_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-Singularity-235">[235]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> has used <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a> (which describes the relentless exponential improvement in digital technology) to calculate that <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">desktop computers</a> will have the same processing power as human brains by the year 2029, and predicts that the singularity will occur in 2045.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singularity_235-2"><a href="#cite_note-Singularity-235">[235]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transhumanism">Transhumanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Transhumanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">Transhumanism</a></div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>You awake one morning to find your brain has another lobe functioning. Invisible, this auxiliary lobe answers your questions with information beyond the realm of your own memory, suggests plausible courses of action, and asks questions that help bring out relevant facts. You quickly come to rely on the new lobe so much that you stop wondering how it works. You just use it. This is the dream of artificial intelligence.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BYTE" title="BYTE">BYTE</a></i>, April 1985<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lemmon198504_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-lemmon198504-236">[236]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Robot designer <a href="/wiki/Hans_Moravec" title="Hans Moravec">Hans Moravec</a>, cyberneticist <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Warwick" title="Kevin Warwick">Kevin Warwick</a> and inventor <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> have predicted that humans and machines will merge in the future into <a href="/wiki/Cyborg" title="Cyborg">cyborgs</a> that are more capable and powerful than either.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transhumanism_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Transhumanism-237">[237]</a></sup> This idea, called <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">transhumanism</a>, which has roots in <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ettinger" title="Robert Ettinger">Robert Ettinger</a>, has been illustrated in fiction as well, for example in the <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ghost_in_the_Shell" title="Ghost in the Shell">Ghost in the Shell</a></i> and the science-fiction series <i><a href="/wiki/Dune_(novel)" title="Dune (novel)">Dune</a></i>.</p>
<p>In the 1980s artist <a href="/wiki/Hajime_Sorayama" title="Hajime Sorayama">Hajime Sorayama</a>'s Sexy Robots series were painted and published in Japan depicting the actual organic human form with lifelike muscular metallic skins and later "the Gynoids" book followed that was used by or influenced movie makers including <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">George Lucas</a> and other creatives. Sorayama never considered these organic robots to be real part of nature but always unnatural product of the human mind, a fantasy existing in the mind even when realized in actual form.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edward_Fredkin" title="Edward Fredkin">Edward Fredkin</a> argues that "artificial intelligence is the next stage in evolution", an idea first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Butler_(novelist)" title="Samuel Butler (novelist)">Samuel Butler</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Darwin_among_the_Machines" title="Darwin among the Machines">Darwin among the Machines</a>" (1863), and expanded upon by <a href="/wiki/George_Dyson_(science_historian)" title="George Dyson (science historian)">George Dyson</a> in his book of the same name in 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AI_as_evolution_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-AI_as_evolution-238">[238]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Existential_risk">Existential risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Existential risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Existential_risk_from_advanced_artificial_intelligence" title="Existential risk from advanced artificial intelligence">Existential risk from advanced artificial intelligence</a></div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The development of full artificial intelligence could spell the end of the human race. Once humans develop artificial intelligence, it will take off on its own and redesign itself at an ever-increasing rate. Humans, who are limited by slow biological evolution, couldn't compete and would be superseded.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>A common concern about the development of artificial intelligence is the potential threat it could pose to mankind. This concern has recently gained attention after mentions by celebrities including <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holley_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-240">[240]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> A group of prominent tech titans including <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thiel" title="Peter Thiel">Peter Thiel</a>, Amazon Web Services and Musk have committed $1billion to <a href="/wiki/OpenAI" title="OpenAI">OpenAI</a> a nonprofit company aimed at championing responsible AI development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> The opinion of experts within the field of artificial intelligence is mixed, with sizable fractions both concerned and unconcerned by risk from eventual superhumanly-capable AI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Superintelligence:_Paths,_Dangers,_Strategies" title="Superintelligence: Paths, Dangers, Strategies">Superintelligence</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Bostrom" title="Nick Bostrom">Nick Bostrom</a> provides an argument that artificial intelligence will pose a threat to mankind. He argues that sufficiently intelligent AI, if it chooses actions based on achieving some goal, will exhibit <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_convergence" title="Instrumental convergence">convergent</a> behavior such as acquiring resources or protecting itself from being shut down. If this AI's goals do not reflect humanity's - one example is an AI told to compute as many digits of pi as possible - it might harm humanity in order to acquire more resources or prevent itself from being shut down, ultimately to better achieve its goal.</p>
<p>For this danger to be realized, the hypothetical AI would have to overpower or out-think all of humanity, which a minority of experts argue is a possibility far enough in the future to not be worth researching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> Other counterarguments revolve around humans being either intrinsically or convergently valuable from the perspective of an artificial intelligence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concern over risk from artificial intelligence has led to some high-profile donations and investments. In January 2015, <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> donated ten million dollars to the <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Life_Institute" title="Future of Life Institute">Future of Life Institute</a> to fund research on understanding AI decision making. The goal of the institute is to "grow wisdom with which we manage" the growing power of technology. Musk also funds companies developing artificial intelligence such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_DeepMind" title="Google DeepMind">Google DeepMind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vicarious_(company)" title="Vicarious (company)">Vicarious</a> to "just keep an eye on what's going on with artificial intelligence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> I think there is potentially a dangerous outcome there."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup></p>
<p>Development of militarized artificial intelligence is a related concern. Currently, 50+ countries are researching battlefield robots, including the United States, China, Russia, and the United Kingdom. Many people concerned about risk from superintelligent AI also want to limit the use of artificial soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral_decision-making">Moral decision-making</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Moral decision-making">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To keep AI ethical, some have suggested teaching new technologies equipped with AI, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Driver-less_car" title="Driver-less car">driver-less cars</a>, to render moral decisions on their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> Others argued that these technologies could learn to act ethically the way children do—by interacting with adults, in particular, with ethicists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> Still others suggest these smart technologies can determine the moral preferences of those who use them (just the way one learns about consumer preferences) and then be programmed to heed these preferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction">In fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: In fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_fiction" title="Artificial intelligence in fiction">Artificial intelligence in fiction</a></div>
<p>The implications of artificial intelligence have been a persistent theme in <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>. Early stories typically revolved around intelligent robots. The word "robot" itself was coined by <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel Čapek">Karel Čapek</a> in his 1921 play <i><a href="/wiki/R.U.R." title="R.U.R.">R.U.R.</a></i>, the title standing for "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rossum%27s_Universal_Robots" title="Rossum's Universal Robots">Rossum's Universal Robots</a>". Later, the SF writer <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Three_Laws_of_Robotics" title="Three Laws of Robotics">Three Laws of Robotics</a> which he subsequently explored in a long series of robot stories. These laws have since gained some traction in genuine AI research.</p>
<p>Other influential fictional intelligences include <a href="/wiki/HAL_9000" title="HAL 9000">HAL</a>, the computer in charge of the spaceship in <i><a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey" title="2001: A Space Odyssey">2001: A Space Odyssey</a></i>, released as both a film and a book in 1968 and written by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>.</p>
<p>AI has since become firmly rooted in popular culture and is in many films, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Terminator" title="The Terminator">The Terminator</a></i> (1984) and <i><a href="/wiki/A.I._Artificial_Intelligence" title="A.I. Artificial Intelligence">A.I. Artificial Intelligence</a></i> (2001).</p>

<li id="cite_note-McCorduck.27s_thesis-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-McCorduck.27s_thesis_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is a central idea of <a href="/wiki/Pamela_McCorduck" title="Pamela McCorduck">Pamela McCorduck</a>'s <i>Machines Who Think</i>. She writes: "I like to think of artificial intelligence as the scientific apotheosis of a venerable cultural tradition." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p. 34) "Artificial intelligence in one form or another is an idea that has pervaded Western intellectual history, a dream in urgent need of being realized." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p. xviii) "Our history is full of attempts—nutty, eerie, comical, earnest, legendary and real—to make artificial intelligences, to reproduce what is the essential us—bypassing the ordinary means. Back and forth between myth and reality, our imaginations supplying what our workshops couldn't, we have engaged for a long time in this odd form of self-reproduction." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p. 3) She traces the desire back to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> roots and calls it the urge to "forge the Gods." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp. 340–400)</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning_41-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Psychological evidence of sub-symbolic reasoning:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWasonShapiro1966">Wason &amp; Shapiro (1966)</a> showed that people do poorly on completely abstract problems, but if the problem is restated to allow the use of intuitive <a href="/wiki/Social_intelligence" title="Social intelligence">social intelligence</a>, performance dramatically improves. (See <a href="/wiki/Wason_selection_task" title="Wason selection task">Wason selection task</a>)</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKahnemanSlovicTversky1982">Kahneman, Slovic &amp; Tversky (1982)</a> have shown that people are terrible at elementary problems that involve uncertain reasoning. (See <a href="/wiki/List_of_cognitive_biases" title="List of cognitive biases">list of cognitive biases</a> for several examples).</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLakoffN.C3.BA.C3.B1ez2000">Lakoff &amp; Núñez (2000)</a> have controversially argued that even our skills at mathematics depend on knowledge and skills that come from "the body", i.e. sensorimotor and perceptual skills. (See <a href="/wiki/Where_Mathematics_Comes_From" title="Where Mathematics Comes From">Where Mathematics Comes From</a>)</span></li>
</ul>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Qualification_problem-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Qualification_problem_49-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Qualification_problem" title="Qualification problem">Qualification problem</a>:</span>
<ul>

<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2011)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
</ul>
While McCarthy was primarily concerned with issues in the logical representation of actions, <a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a> apply the term to the more general issue of default reasoning in the vast network of assumptions underlying all our commonsense knowledge.</li>
<li id="cite_note-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_50-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_50-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Default reasoning and <a href="/wiki/Default_logic" title="Default logic">default logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-monotonic_logic" title="Non-monotonic logic">non-monotonic logics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circumscription_(logic)" title="Circumscription (logic)">circumscription</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_world_assumption" title="Closed world assumption">closed world assumption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">abduction</a> (Poole <i>et al.</i> places abduction under "default reasoning". Luger <i>et al.</i> places this under "uncertain reasoning"):</span>

<li id="cite_note-Intuition-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Intuition_54-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Intuition_54-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Expert knowledge as <a href="/wiki/Embodied_cognition" title="Embodied cognition">embodied</a> intuition:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDreyfusDreyfus1986">Dreyfus &amp; Dreyfus 1986</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus" title="Hubert Dreyfus">Hubert Dreyfus</a> is a philosopher and critic of AI who was among the first to argue that most useful human knowledge was encoded sub-symbolically. <li id="cite_note-Planning-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Planning_55-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">Planning</a>:</span>

Mitchell">Tom Mitchell</a>'s widely quoted definition of machine learning: "A computer program is set to learn from an experience <i>E</i> with respect to some task <i>T</i> and some performance measure <i>P</i> if its performance on <i>T</i> as measured by <i>P</i> improves with experience <i>E</i>."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Emotion_and_affective_computing-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Emotion_and_affective_computing_88-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Emotion and <a href="/wiki/Affective_computing" title="Affective computing">affective computing</a>:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMinsky2006">Minsky 2006</a></span></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Artificial_consciousness-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Artificial_consciousness_89-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Gerald_Edelman" title="Gerald Edelman">Gerald Edelman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Igor_Aleksander" title="Igor Aleksander">Igor Aleksander</a> and others have argued that <a href="/wiki/Artificial_consciousness" title="Artificial consciousness">artificial consciousness</a> is required for strong AI. (<a href="#CITEREFAleksander1995">Aleksander 1995</a>; <a href="#CITEREFEdelman2007">Edelman 2007</a>)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Brain_simulation-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_simulation_90-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_simulation_90-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">Artificial brain</a> arguments: AI requires a simulation of the operation of the human brain</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, p. 957</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp. 271 and 279</span></li>
</ul>
<span class="reference-text">A few of the people who make some form of the argument:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMoravec1988">Moravec 1988</a></span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKurzweil2005">Kurzweil 2005</a>, p. 262</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHawkinsBlakeslee2005">Hawkins &amp; Blakeslee 2005</a></span></li>
</ul>
<span class="reference-text">The most extreme form of this argument (the brain replacement scenario) was put forward by <a href="/wiki/Clark_Glymour" title="Clark Glymour">Clark Glymour</a> in the mid-1970s and was touched on by <a href="/wiki/Zenon_Pylyshyn" title="Zenon Pylyshyn">Zenon Pylyshyn</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Searle" title="John Searle">John Searle</a> in 1980.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-AI_complete-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-AI_complete_91-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AI_complete" title="AI complete">AI complete</a>: <a href="#CITEREFShapiro1992">Shapiro 1992</a>, p. 9</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nils_Nilsson_(researcher)" title="Nils Nilsson (researcher)">Nils Nilsson</a> writes: "Simply put, there is wide disagreement in the field about what AI is all about" (<a href="#CITEREFNilsson1983">Nilsson 1983</a>, p. 10).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_93-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_93-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Biological intelligence vs. intelligence in general:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp. 2–3, who make the analogy with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeronautical_engineering" title="Aeronautical engineering">aeronautical engineering</a>.</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp. 100–101, who writes that there are "two major branches of artificial intelligence: one aimed at producing intelligent behavior regardless of how it was accomplioshed, and the other aimed at modeling intelligent processes found in nature, particularly human ones."</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKolata1982">Kolata 1982</a>, a paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, which describes <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">McCarthy's</a> indifference to biological models. Kolata quotes McCarthy as writing: "This is AI, so we don't care if it's psychologically real"<a class="external autonumber" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=PEkqAAAAMAAJ&amp;q=%22we+don't+care+if+it's+psychologically+real%22&amp;dq=%22we+don't+care+if+it's+psychologically+real%22&amp;output=html&amp;pgis=1" rel="nofollow">[1]</a>. McCarthy recently reiterated his position at the <a href="/wiki/AI@50" title="AI@50">AI@50</a> conference where he said "Artificial intelligence is not, by definition, simulation of human intelligence" (<a href="#CITEREFMaker2006">Maker 2006</a>).</span></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_94-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_94-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" title="Neats vs. scruffies">Neats vs. scruffies</a>:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp. 421–424, 486–489</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp. 168</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFNilsson1983">Nilsson 1983</a>, pp. 10–11</span></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_95-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_95-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Symbolic vs. sub-symbolic AI:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFNilsson1998">Nilsson (1998</a>, p. 7), who uses the term "sub-symbolic".</span></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaugeland1985255-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaugeland1985255_96-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHaugeland1985">Haugeland 1985</a>, p. 255.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTELaw1994-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaw1994_97-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLaw1994">Law 1994</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBach2008-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBach2008_98-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBach2008">Bach 2008</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-GOFAI-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-GOFAI_99-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHaugeland1985">Haugeland 1985</a>, pp. 112–117</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The most dramatic case of sub-symbolic AI being pushed into the background was the devastating critique of <a href="/wiki/Perceptron" title="Perceptron">perceptrons</a> by <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Papert" title="Seymour Papert">Seymour Papert</a> in 1969. <li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dreyfus criticized the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necessary_and_sufficient" title="Necessary and sufficient">necessary</a> condition of the <a href="/wiki/Physical_symbol_system" title="Physical symbol system">physical symbol system</a> hypothesis, which he called the "psychological assumption": "The mind can be viewed as a device operating on bits of information according to formal rules". (<a href="#CITEREFDreyfus1992">Dreyfus 1992</a>, p. 156)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Dreyfus.27_critique-208"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dreyfus.27_critique_208-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreyfus%27_critique_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Dreyfus' critique of artificial intelligence">Dreyfus' critique of artificial intelligence</a>:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDreyfus1972">Dreyfus 1972</a>, <a href="#CITEREFDreyfusDreyfus1986">Dreyfus &amp; Dreyfus 1986</a></span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp. 120–132</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp. 211–239</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp. 950–952,</span></li>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-G.C3.B6del_himself-209"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-G.C3.B6del_himself_209-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFG.C3.B6del1951">Gödel 1951</a>: in this lecture, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del" title="Kurt Gödel">Kurt Gödel</a> uses the incompleteness theorem to arrive at the following disjunction: (a) the human mind is not a consistent finite machine, or (b) there exist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diophantine_equations" title="Diophantine equations">Diophantine equations</a> for which it cannot decide whether solutions exist. Gödel finds (b) implausible, and thus seems to have believed the human mind was not equivalent to a finite machine, i.e., its power exceeded that of any finite machine. He recognized that this was only a conjecture, since one could never disprove (b). Yet he considered the disjunctive conclusion to be a "certain fact".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-The_mathematical_objection-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-The_mathematical_objection_210-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Mathematical Objection:</span>

<li id="cite_note-229"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-229">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Steven_Horst&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Steven Horst (page does not exist)">Horst, Steven</a>, (2005) <a class="external text" href="http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/computational-mind/" rel="nofollow">"The Computational Theory of Mind"</a> in <i>The Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Searle.27s_strong_AI-230"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Searle.27s_strong_AI_230-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This version is from <a href="#CITEREFSearle1999">Searle (1999)</a>, and is also quoted in <a href="#CITEREFDennett1991">Dennett 1991</a>, p. 435. Searle's original formulation was "The appropriately programmed computer really is a mind, in the sense that computers given the right programs can be literally said to understand and have other cognitive states." (<a href="#CITEREFSearle1980">Searle 1980</a>, p. 1). Strong AI is defined similarly by <a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig (2003</a>, p. 947): "The assertion that machines could possibly act intelligently (or, perhaps better, act as if they were intelligent) is called the 'weak AI' hypothesis by philosophers, and the assertion that machines that do so are actually thinking (as opposed to simulating thinking) is called the 'strong AI' hypothesis."</span></li>

<span class="reference-text">In fiction:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck (2004</a>, p. 190-25) discusses <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i> and identifies the key ethical issues as scientific hubris and the suffering of the monster, i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robot_rights" title="Robot rights">robot rights</a>.</span></li>
</ul>
</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="AI_textbooks">AI textbooks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: AI textbooks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="refbegin" style="">

<li><a class="external text" href="http://aitopics.org/" rel="nofollow">AITopics</a> – A large directory of links and other resources maintained by the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Artificial_Intelligence" title="Association for the Advancement of Artificial Intelligence">Association for the Advancement of Artificial Intelligence</a>, the leading organization of academic AI researchers.</li>
